# 1\. Introduction

Razorpay API is one of the major product touch points through which our customers interface with us. In fact, other than dashboard and checkout, APIs are the default communication mechanism between Razorpay and the client system.  
Sooner or later, you will end up building and modifying aspects of our API footprint across the products. This guide is intended to help you in that process. The aim is to list down the basic Do's and Don'ts of working with and designing a RESTFul API, especially in the context of Razorpay.  
Note, that this document is not a basic primer on REST APIs. There will be numerous tutorials on that available on the internet. For example \-

* [Representational state transfer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Representational_state_transfer)  
* [What is REST — A Simple Explanation for Beginners, Part 1: Introduction](https://medium.com/extend/what-is-rest-a-simple-explanation-for-beginners-part-1-introduction-b4a072f8740f)

The aim of this document is to detail the thought process around how to go about designing APIs. If you don't know what REST is, then please go through the above links first to have a basic understanding of the same and then return to read the rest of the document.

# 2\. What is REST?

REST stands for REpresentational State Transfer. **It means when a RESTful API is called, the server will transfer to the client a representation of the state of the requested resource.**   
Web services that conform to the REST architectural style are called RESTful Web services (RWS). You will hear similar sentences a lot \- "This API is not RESTFul, please rework it."  
From wikipedia \- "**Representational state transfer** (**REST**) is a [software architectural](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Software_architecture) style that defines a set of constraints to be used for creating [Web services](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_service). Web services that conform to the REST architectural style, called *RESTful* Web services, provide interoperability between computer systems on the [Internet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet). RESTful Web services allow the requesting systems to access and manipulate textual representations of [Web resources](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_resource) by using a uniform and predefined set of [stateless](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stateless_protocol) operations. Other kinds of Web services, such as [SOAP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SOAP) Web services, expose their own arbitrary sets of operations."  
Let's identify a few key attributes of REST from above definition \-

* Stateless  
* All the RESTFul operations are expected to be stateless. Statelessness means that every HTTP request happens in complete isolation. When the client makes an HTTP request, it includes all the information necessary for the server to fulfill that request. The server never relies on information from previous requests. If that information was important, the client would have to send it again in subsequent request.  
* This doesn't mean that the server is stateless. The server holds the complete state required for it to complete the information but the client here is stateless.  
* To illustrate with an example, when you login into a service, the subsequent client requests rely on the previous logged in state when talking to the server, and the server needs to be aware of this. On the other hand, in the case of RESTFul API calls which are stateless, the authentication information needs to be passed in with every single request.  
* Interoperability  
* Uniform and Predefined set of operations

# 3\. Entity Model

Entities are at the core of a RESTFul API design. The very first step when designing the api from scratch is to identify the entities also called API **resources.**  
Every API resource represents an Entity which often corresponds to a real world corollary.  
A payment is an entity and so is a refund as well as a merchant and an invoice.

## 3.1. Creating API URIs

After creating an entity, the next step is to design the API URIs. If you are wondering what's the difference between URL and URI \- a URI is an identifier but a URL is an identifier that tells you how to get to it. (Read more [here](https://danielmiessler.com/study/url-uri/)).  
In Razorpay, a **payment** is a top level API entity/resource. The different resource URIs will look like this \-

| /payments/payments/{payment\_id} |
| :---- |

## 3.2. HTTP Methods

The URIs that we have mentioned till now do not contain any verb. In REST, the action involved is denoted by the HTTP method. The API URI should have a direct relation with the entity or the product. The first entity in the URL must be a noun and plural.  
Below is the list of HTTP methods \-

| GET | To get a resource |
| :---- | :---- |
| POST | To create a resource |
| PUT | To replace an existing resource |
| PATCH | To modify an existing resource |
| DELETE | To delete an existing resource |
| OPTIONS | Not used |
| TRACE | Not used |
| CONNECT | Not used |
| HEAD | Not used |

 

## 3.3. HTTP Response Status Codes

HTTP Response status codes indicate whether a specific [HTTP](https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/HTTP) request has been successfully completed. Responses are grouped in five classes:

* [Informational responses](https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/HTTP/Status#information_responses) (100–199)  
* [Successful responses](https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/HTTP/Status#successful_responses) (200–299)  
* [Redirection messages](https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/HTTP/Status#redirection_messages) (300–399)  
* [Client error responses](https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/HTTP/Status#client_error_responses) (400–499)  
* [Server error responses](https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/HTTP/Status#server_error_responses) (500–599)

You can read more about each of the response status codes in the Mozilla Docs.  
We will document the response status codes most relevant to a RESTFul API and ones which you are most likely to encounter. Most of the status code text below is directly copied from Mozilla Docs.  
**200 OK**  
The request succeeded. The result meaning of "success" depends on the HTTP method:

* GET: The resource has been fetched and transmitted in the message body.  
* HEAD: The representation headers are included in the response without any message body.  
* PUT or POST: The resource describing the result of the action is transmitted in the message body.  
* TRACE: The message body contains the request message as received by the server.

**201 Created**  
The request succeeded, and a new resource was created as a result. This is typically the response sent after POST requests, or some PUT requests.  
**400 Bad Request**  
The server could not understand the request due to invalid syntax.  
**401 Unauthorized**  
Although the HTTP standard specifies "unauthorized", semantically this response means "unauthenticated". That is, the client must authenticate itself to get the requested response.  
**404 Not Found**  
The server can not find the requested resource. In the browser, this means the URL is not recognized. In an API, this can also mean that the endpoint is valid but the resource itself does not exist. Servers may also send this response instead of 403 Forbidden to hide the existence of a resource from an unauthorized client. This response code is probably the most well known due to its frequent occurrence on the web.  
**500 Internal Server Error**  
The server has encountered a situation it does not know how to handle.  
**502 Bad Gateway**  
This error response means that the server, while working as a gateway to get a response needed to handle the request, got an invalid response.  
**503 Service Unavailable**  
The server is not ready to handle the request. Common causes are a server that is down for maintenance or that is overloaded. Note that together with this response, a user-friendly page explaining the problem should be sent. This response should be used for temporary conditions and the Retry-After HTTP header should, if possible, contain the estimated time before the recovery of the service. The webmaster must also take care about the caching-related headers that are sent along with this response, as these temporary condition responses should usually not be cached.  
**504 Gateway Timeout**  
This error response is given when the server is acting as a gateway and cannot get a response in time.

## 3.3. Representation \- JSON vs XML

Almost all our APIs return JSON content by default. In the early stages, there was a thought around designing our APIs in XML as that's what most of the banks supported. However, today most of the new generation developers intuitively support and understand JSON. There will be enterprise developers who prefer XML over JSON still, but they are also beginning to migrate and JSON definitely resonates better in our TG.  
JSON by default is fine, but at some point in the future, we might support XML input/output as well. Specifically, HTTP provides a header parameter called Accept which the client (a server or a browser) can use to define what format it expects in the response[\[1\]](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#ftnt1). This is what we should use as well, to decide what format to send the response in.  
In future, we may look to introduce other formats for API paradigm if the need arises. GraphQL by Facebook has been a recent phenomenon and has a very well defined structure for quite a few use-cases. Many companies like Github have migrated to it. The good thing about GraphQL is that it plays pretty well with RESTFul APIs as well.

## 3.4. Uniform Representation

All actions and methods must return exactly the same response. What does this mean? You may be tempted to send back different responses for a GET action vs a POST action i.e. when you are fetching an entity vs when creating an entity. But having a uniform, predictable response is inherently a superior experience compared to any variability. The developer doesn't need to second guess himself and refer back to documentation just to understand the response structure he will be getting back and which attribute is present in the response of what action. This is also one of the core tenets of RESTFul API Design. This same principle applies to webhooks as well.  
*Note: Webhooks are not yet discussed in this document.*

## 3.5. Versioning

Today all our APIs are by default versioned as /v1. So payments apis are denoted as /v1/payments. This is to ensure that any massive breakage of payments apis in future can have smooth migration. We can go to /v1.1 or directly to /v2. The /v1.1 looks ugly so hopefully we will avoid that or find a different representation of the same.

## 3.6. JSON

From Wikipedia, **JSON** (**JavaScript Object Notation**, pronounced [/ˈdʒeɪsən/](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:IPA/English); also [/ˈdʒeɪˌsɒn/](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:IPA/English)) is an [open standard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Open_standard) [file format](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File_format) and [data interchange](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electronic_data_interchange) format that uses [human-readable](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human-readable_medium) text to store and transmit data objects consisting of [attribute–value pairs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Attribute%E2%80%93value_pair) and [arrays](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Array_data_type) (or other [serializable](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Serialization) values). It is a common [data](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Data) format with diverse uses in [electronic data interchange](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electronic_data_interchange), including that of [web applications](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Web_application) with [servers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Server_\(computing\)).  
JSON is a [language-independent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Language-independent_specification) data format. It was derived from [JavaScript](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/JavaScript), but many modern [programming languages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Programming_language) include code to generate and [parse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parsing) JSON-format data. JSON filenames use the extension **.json**.  
[Douglas Crockford](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Douglas_Crockford) originally specified the JSON format in the early 2000s.

### 3.6.1. Syntax

The following example shows a possible JSON representation describing a person.

| { "first\_name": "John", "last\_name": "Smith", "is\_alive": true, "age": 27, "address": {   "street\_address": "21 2nd Street",   "city": "New York",   "state": "NY",   "postal\_code": "10021-3100" }, "phone\_numbers": \[   {     "type": "home",     "number": "212 555-1234"   },   {     "type": "office",     "number": "646 555-4567"   } \], "children": \[\], "spouse": null} |
| :---- |

This is a quick and simple example. The format is generally set up with two curly braces (or curly brackets) that look like this { } on either end of it, and with key-value pairs populating the space between. Most data used in JSON ends up being encapsulated in a JSON object.

### 3.6.2. JSON Key Value Pairs

Key-value pairs have a colon between them as in "key" : "value". Each key-value pair is separated by a comma, so the middle of a JSON looks like this: "key" : "value", "key" : "value", "key": "value". In our example above, the first key-value pair is "first\_name": "John".  
JSON **keys** are on the left side of the colon. They need to be wrapped in double quotation marks, as in "key", and can be any valid string. Within each object, keys need to be unique. These key strings *can* include whitespaces, as in "first\_name", but that can make it harder to access when you’re programming, so it’s best to use underscores, as in "first\_name".  
JSON **values** are found to the right of the colon.

### 3.6.3. Data Types

Each value in a JSON key value pair can be of following data types \-

* Boolean  
* Integer  
* Float  
* String  
* Array  
* Object/Dictionary  
* Entity

An attribute's type should not change from one response to another and must always have the values of the same type. Not adhering to this is in some sense breaking the API contract and creates problems specifically in static languages.

### **3.6.4. Usage of null**

In JSON and in most programming languages, null is a special value. For starters, null doesn't have a type as defined above. It by default denotes an absence of any kind of value.  
All attributes except objects/dictionaries can support null value along with their default type. We just talked about how an attribute's type cannot change even with different values. But null is a special case and an attribute having null value does not change it's type. In a way, most programming languages create an exception for null value and accord it special status.  
However, an object or dictionary can be empty but must not ever be null. A lot of people designing APIs make this mistake. More on this later.

### 3.6.5. JSON Object Structure

A JSON object or entity contains zero, one, or more key-value pairs, also called properties. The object is surrounded by curly braces {}. Every key-value pair is separated by a comma. The order of the key-value pair is irrelevant.

### 3.6.6. JSON Array Structure

A JSON array contains zero, one, or more ordered elements, separated by a comma. The JSON array is surrounded by square brackets \[ \].  
A JSON array is zero terminated, the first index of the array is zero (0). Therefore, the last index of the array is length \- 1\.  
The elements of a JSON array can be of different [data types](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#h.ovxvivknm0b2).

### 3.6.7. Array and Object

In REST, you will find types array and list being interchangeably used. They mean the same. Similarly, the terms object and dictionary are also interchangeably used and mean the same. An attribute can be of type array or object but cannot be both.  
An Array starts and ends with '\[' and '\]'. Between them, a number of values can reside. If there are more than one values residing, they are separated by ','.  
An object starts and ends with '{' and '}'. Between them, a number of key value pairs can reside. Key and value is separated by a ':' and if there are more than one key value pairs, they are separated by ','. A key is always of type string while a value can be of any type and can even be nested.  
JSON array is a collection of **ordered** **values** with \[\], while JSON object is collection of **unordered key, value pair** with curly braces {}.  
Also note the the importance of difference in ordering of data in both cases. In case of an array, whenever you fetch data, it must always be similarly ordered. If the order changes then the data could be considered changed. Which is not the case for an object.  
Following is an example of an object.

| { "first\_name": "Bidhan",   "last\_name": "Chatterjee",   "age": 40,   "email":"bidhan@example.com" } |
| :---- |

#### 3.6.7.1. Nested Arrays and Objects

In JSON, objects can nest arrays (starts and ends with '\[' and '\]') within it. The following example shows that.

| {   "students": \[     {       "name": "Amit Goenka",       "major":"Physics"     },     {       "name": "Smita Pallod",       "major":"Chemistry"      },     {       "name":"Rajeev Sen",       "major":"Mathematics"     }                             \] } |
| :---- |

If the JSON data describes an array, then each element of that array is an object.

| \[   {     "name": "Harshil Mathur",     "email": "harshil@example.com"   },   {     "name": "Abhay Rana",     "email": "nemo@razorpay.com"   } \] |
| :---- |

Even arrays can also be nested within an object. The following shows that.

| {   "first\_name": "Abhay",   "last\_name": "Rana",   "age": 30,   "address": {      "street\_addres": "224 B, Baker Street",      "city": "London",      "state": "London",      "pincode": "100000"   },   "phone": \[      {        "type": "personal",        "number": "09832209761"      },      {        "type": "fax",        "number": "91-342-2567692"      }   \] } |
| :---- |

#### 3.6.7.2. Empty Objects or Arrays

As mentioned previously, objects or arrays can never be null. However, they can be empty.  
**This is more of a good convention than syntactical JSON rule.** You can make it null but then that causes problems in quite a few static programming languages which have the philosophy of never having an object/dict as null.  
Why is that the case? Well, in many programming languages (especially Java, etc.), you have to define the attribute type explicitly at the time of declaration. And that type shouldn't change over time. However, all types accept null as a special value which is not of the same type. But this is not true for objects and arrays. Most languages do not recognize null as a special value for these attribute types.  
We will still encounter scenarios where there is no value in the object or array and that's fine. We can have empty arrays or objects as shown below \-  
Empty Object \-  {}  
Empty Array \- \[\]  
A common question is whether we should send the attributes when the values are empty. Yes, we should because the values being empty also has a definite meaning. Versus if the attributes are missed out then it violates quite a few principles that we have defined in this doc. There is a scenario of dynamic attributes that we will tackle later. But for beginners, go with the understanding that attributes with empty values should always be sent.

#### 3.6.7.3. Other obscure terms for objects or arrays

Most attribute types have simple names and are consistent across various texts. However, while reading public documentation of various companies you may encounter different names for objects and arrays which you should be aware about. Some of these have unique properties which are usually a subset of object/array behaviour.

* Object  
* Associative Arrays  
* Hashmap  
* Map  
* Array  
* Vector (one dimensional array)  
* Matrix (Two dimensional array)

# **4\. Designing API[\[a\]](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#cmnt1)[\[b\]](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#cmnt2)[\[c\]](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#cmnt3)[\[d\]](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#cmnt4)**

[\[e\]](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#cmnt5)[\[f\]](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#cmnt6)  
Now that we have covered the basics, we will talk about some of the usual patterns to keep in mind when you go about writing a new API.

## 4.1. Longevity

The one important thing about designing APIs compared to a product with an interface is that APIs are by definition supposed to be long lived. You usually don't have the option of breaking things easily and quickly, and migration between versions is always a bit of a pain. Compare this to a dashboard or an app, where you can have a new release up in a week in case any interface is not working great. That's why it's important to have a long term outlook and philosophy when designing APIs. A good corollary for this scenario is "Move slow and deliberate" when designing APIs compared to "Move Fast and Break Things" (from Facebook).

### **4.1.1. Breaking Change[\[g\]](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#cmnt7)**

There have been mentions of breaking changes of API contracts in this doc. This refers to any unexpected behaviour by a server that renders some of its functionality unusable for an existing client.  
A simple example of this is our payment capture API. Initially we expected our merchants to send the payment amount in the payload for this API, and nothing else. With multi-currency support, it became important that the merchant send us the payment currency as well. If we simply updated our logic to always expect a field called ‘currency’ to be present in the input, that would constitute a breaking change, since many of our older customers would not be sending the new field, and their requests would fail. For this reason, we have assumed the currency is INR by default, and do not expect it to be sent in the input for INR payments.  
That was an example of breaking change in the request, but discussions around breaking changes in response are far more common. Notably, we would cause a breaking change if we ever took an existing attribute in a response and changed its name or type, since there are likely clients out there who have integrated the API assuming the older name/type.  
More subtly, breaking changes can be caused by a change in behaviour. Eg. Currently the status of our payments remains captured even when the merchant has done a partial refund on it. It is only when he refunds the entire amount that the payment moves to the refunded state. In the future, if we changed the behaviour so that the payment is called refunded after a single partial refund, that too would constitute a breaking change.  
All our API responses are in JSON, and JSON is defined as a dictionary-type data structure. This means that **while removal or change of existing attributes could cause issues for the client, the addition of new attributes does not**. In fact, even changing the order of existing attributes is not technically a breakage for a dictionary-type data structure[\[2\]](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#ftnt2).  
Any change to an API that causes a breaking change is called backward-incompatible.

## 4.2. Readability

I believe WhatsApp product philosophy is absolutely applicable in this case. A reader should easily be able to parse the APIs in one read or use. And remember how to use it later as well. If it requires copious amounts of documentation to explain a simple thing then it's probably not good. When product managers hand-off API specs to engineers, they should be as much of a finished spec as possible. The engineers and the PMs should be closely involved in designing the APIs so that there’s joint ownership. But it's still the PM's responsibility to do a proper handover of the product spec documenting all the APIs to the engineering team.  
Secondly, a lot of PMs think that it's the engineer's job to write the APIs as it's slightly technical in nature. Quite a few product managers don't get the point of fretting over attribute names or the exact API structure. For quite a few PMs, especially the ones from non-technical backgrounds, this seems to be quite a cumbersome and challenging job on the first try and most tend to gloss over it. Quite a few write basic structures hoping that it will pass muster and probably just be good enough.  
In fact, one of the reasons for writing this doc is to document all the best practices and edge cases because we have been through the process of explaining the importance of designing APIs and how to go about it with every single PM and engineer who has joined Razorpay and who has fumbled extensively in their first try. PMs are better suited to do this job because they are always trained to think from a customer's POV which is not necessarily the case with engineers. Secondly, PMs need to worry about breakages, backward compatibility and other customer centric things much more because ultimately, they are supposed to manage these processes and end customer communications. This is not to say that engineers are not expected to also contribute to this, in fact, good engineers take pride in designing excellent APIs. But it's been challenging to build this as a core competency in engineering especially at junior levels and the thought process only matures over time. It's better suited to be done by PMs in collaboration with engineering. In the end, if a customer does not like the interface, it will be a problem that we will have to solve and be responsible for.

## 4.3. Attribute Names

An important part of designing APIs is to keep it as simple as possible and not complicate it further unless absolutely necessary.  
For example, an attribute can contain the list of a few transactions.

| "xxx": { txn1, txn2, txn3 } |
| :---- |

If you ask any newbie engineer designing APIs, most (not all) of them would name "xxx" as "txnList" or "allTxns" or "allTransactions" because it's descriptive but also because that's how you would name variables in code. However, for a lot of people reading this is not intuitive and aesthetically unappealing. The attribute name "transactions" does as good a job of conveying the meaning here. In fact, even product managers with technical background make the same mistake. And product managers with non-technical background don't get the point of fretting over an attribute name.  
It's important to capture proper functionality and weigh trade-offs between making APIs verbose to improve readability vs maximum functionality.

### 4.3.1. Underscore in Attribute Names

When you simply cannot avoid having two words in an attribute name, then those words are joined by an underscore. Why underscore and not a hyphen or simply camel case or even anything else?  
This goes towards the naming convention of variables in programming languages. There are two major conventions that are supported \- snake case and camel case. Snake case means variables with two words are joined by an underscore while camel case means variables with two words are joined via capitalisation of the second word. We chose snake case as a convention and have stuck with it since then. There is no major advantage between the two but sticking with one long term is important.  
We didn't choose hyphen or anything else because in many languages, support for hyphen and other special characters is either really poor or outrightly now allowed.  
You may wonder that attribute names are typical strings then where does the variable aspect of it come into picture? That's because json attribute names get directly translated to object attributes or object properties in many languages. For example, in PHP, you can do $payment-\>payment\_link and get it's value. The attribute "payment\_link" is really a string key in json but becomes an object here. A lot of devs simply want to copy paste code for integration and these minor things are important to provide a good experience.

### 4.3.2. Short forms in Attribute Names

By now you would understand that short names like "txn", "pmnt" etc. are **absolutely never preferable**. Use full names instead to ensure better readability. A payment\_link is much more readable than calling it pl which can refer to personal loan or premier league\! There is no exception here.

### 4.3.3. Acronyms in Attribute Names

Mostly, we do not recommend to use acronyms. The specific reason is because most acronyms are obscure in nature and are not easily understood by readers.  
However, sometimes using acronyms are okay if acronyms have become publicly accepted in conversations like ifsc or iin. However, otherwise it's not advisable to use acronyms as most acronyms are obscure in nature.

### 4.3.4. Industry Convention in Attribute Names

Industry convention is often not a good enough reason to decide attribute names. We have changed industry conventions multiple times with fresh thinking and outside perspective often helps. Also, industry conventions often do not carry well across countries. Hence an industry convention name that might work in India may not work in the US and vice versa. Also, industry convention may not make it easy for a new developer or PM to understand the functionality quickly and have good readability.  
4.3.3. Razorpay in Attribute Names  
We also do not use razorpay or rzp in our function names or attribute names as it will never make sense. Please avoid these basic naming issues.

### 4.3.4. Identifier Attributes

All entities generally have an identifier attribute called "id".  In our case, it's a 14 character generated identifier. It's generated using a Razorpay specific algorithm.  
[\[h\]](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#cmnt8)[\[i\]](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#cmnt9)  
The customer identifier in customer entity is referred to as id. However, payment identifier if hypothetically present in customer entity is referred to as payment\_id.  
And, vice versa, payment identifier in payment entity is call id but the customer identifier if present in payment entity is called as customer\_id. This is the general naming convention that we follow. You can read more about identifiers in [Common Attributes \- Identifier](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#kix.gvs3ls9xmkt0)

### 4.3.5. Reference Attributes

A generic liking that most PMs and engineers have is naming attributes as "reference". This, we have found, is never a good idea because the attribute name is too generic to convey any meaning whatsoever. It's usually better to find a name for the attribute that correlates to it's usage in some sense. So, never do "reference", "bankReference", "txnReference", "external\_id" and so on, unless it absolutely makes sense. Usually, alternate names are always better.  
However, only for exactly one use case, over time, we have come to standardising on using the "reference\_id" attribute name. This is when we want to refer to the identifier provided by the merchant for an attribute. For example, in the customer entity, if the merchant wants to provide their own reference, then we store it in the reference\_id attribute.  
This is not always the case though. In payments and orders, we have used receipt as the parameter to store merchant identifiers for those entities as they seem more apt in that context.

## **4.4. Actions and URLs**

We have already read through the basics of HTTP methods in the earlier section [3.2. HTTP Methods](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#h.n3666oxsgaay). The point of defining an entity is so that you can perform actions on it. An entity without any action is pretty much useless. The most common action is simply fetch or read or get with the associated HTTP action GET. The second most common action is the creation of an entity e.g., creating a payment or payout etc. And this has the associated HTTP verb POST.

### 4.4.1 Designing a new Entity URL

In Razorpay, a **payment** is a top level API entity/resource. The different resource URIs will look like this \-

| /payments/payments/{payment\_id} |
| :---- |

If you want to design an API for Payments, then the url should be  
GET /payments for fetching all payments and  
GET /payments/{payment\_id} for fetching a specific payment.  
The response of all such fetches must be [uniform](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#h.5slptkjur655).  
You will read more about fetching entities with specific query parameters at a later topic \- [4.8 Querying and filters](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#h.2ikiqfwds58j)  
Notice that the API for fetching multiple payments is GET /payments. Do not do /getPayments or /fetchPayments or /retrievePayments or /payments/details. The verb is implicit based on the GET HTTP verb when hitting the url. The behaviour of the API changes based on the verb even when the URL is the same. Eg. POST /refunds creates a new refund, but GET /refunds fetches existing refunds.  
If any action needs to be performed beyond these basic actions offered by HTTP, then we can put the action at the end to get the action specific URI.  
eg, to cancel a subscription, the URL will look like \-  
POST /subscriptions/{subscription\_id}/cancel[\[j\]](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#cmnt10)

**All other actions are always POST and not any other HTTP verb as POST by default denotes a change in the resource.**  
**We typically never use PUT because we never think about fully replacing resource attributes wholesale. For modifying or updating a resource, PATCH works fine.**  
Get all payments

| HTTP GET /payments |
| :---- |

Get a single payment

| HTTP GET /payments/{payment\_id} |
| :---- |

Get refunds of a payment

| HTTP GET /refunds?payment\_id={payment\_id} |
| :---- |

Cancel a subscription

| HTTP POST /subscriptions/{subscription\_id}/cancel |
| :---- |

Modify an existing subscription

| HTTP PATCH /subscriptions/{subscription\_id} |
| :---- |

Delete a Payment Page

| HTTP DELETE /payment\_pages/{paymentpage\_id} |
| :---- |

#### 4.1.1.1. Why Plurals?

Plural because it denotes multiple by default. It denotes singular when a particular id is specified.  
You can probably use singular as well. There are projects out there which would have gone that way but all our APIs now follow the same convention. It also helps avoid one more url of /payment/all because it gets covered by default and is aesthetically appealing.

### 4.4.2. Designing a new action URL

You will run into situations where you need to design a new action for an entity beyond the basics of [CRUD](https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Glossary/CRUD) (create, read, update, delete). For any new action, think of the simplest ways of designing the url such that it conveys the meaning of that action.  
As we discussed before, creating a new subscription is POST /subscriptions. Getting a subscription URL will be GET /subscriptions.  
Now, suppose you want to go ahead and cancel an existing subscription, then what it's URL should look like? The most preferred answer according to our philosophy (and REST) should be POST /subscriptions/{subscription\_id}/cancel.  
Let's unpack how we reached this and define simple rules for designing a new action URL \-  

* Method is always POST \- The HTTP method is POST because we are changing the state of the resource via an action and all changes (except update and delete) are done via the POST method.  
* URL format \- The general URL format for any action is extremely simple \- /entity/{entity\_id}/action. There are no exceptions to this rule.  
* Defining the action \- The action word itself should be very simple and direct. In case of canceling a subscription the action is cancel and hence the URL looks like POST /subscriptions/{subscription\_id}/cancel. Note that it's not cancelled or pleaseCancel or to\_be\_cancelled. As much as possible, we should avoid double words or complicated verbs. The verb should be simple and self-explanatory in nature. Most preferred will be single word verbs but joint words are acceptable in scenarios where it will make sense. I am not aware of any example of an action which has two words in the Razorpay universe at the moment. There are entities which are made up of more than one word, but no actions.

### 4.4.3. When to design a new action?

This is an important but simple question. When should you design a new action? Simply put, it should be whenever the existing HTTP verbs and already defined custom actions do not cover the new functionality that needs to be added.  
A new action though should not be introduced when input to an existing action is changing significantly. It should also not be added in case one action can cause multiple changes when the end result is the same.  
Note, that unfortunately, we haven't followed our own advice especially when designing payment creation APIs where we have added multiple URLs for the same creation task. However, that has happened due to lack of our understanding and is one of the reasons why we have this guide in place now.  
Let's take an example \- the default payout creation api is POST /payouts. Now, a payout creation happens on the basis of the inputs fund\_account\_id and contact\_id. Both fund account and contact first need to be created before payout can be created.  
So, the sequence of operations will be \-  
POST /contacts  
POST /fund\_accounts  
POST /payouts  
Noticeably, a payout creation requires 3 api calls. Now, a lot of customers would ask for payout creation in a single api call. The product team hence then decides to do a payout creation in a single api call. To do this, we can take the fund account and contact related input in the same request. And we can decide to create the contact and fund\_account entities in the background using the input provided in the payout creation call itself.  
In this scenario, the input to payout will need to change significantly from the earlier version. One way to solve this is via creating a new composite api. Basically, we design a new URL which will accept a different set of input. For example, if the current URL is POST /payouts, now we can design a new URL POST /payouts/composite. Composite is one of the favorite words internally because you are in a sense doing a composite operation of contact, fund\_account and payout creation in one call.  
However, almost always, we reject this solution for the same reasons[\[k\]](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#cmnt11). The fact is, the new URL has the same purpose as the old one \- that is creating a payout. In REST, we usually prefer to have a single URL for the same operation even if the operation itself can happen via different kinds of inputs or flows. Also, this has a certain kind of implication on the public language SDKs and public docs. Usually, you have one sdk function or doc heading for a certain operation. Splitting it into multiple functions and doc headers doesn't look ideal.  
Another example is payment \- payment flows differ a lot for card vs upi vs netbanking etc. But the api calls are pretty uniform for all with slight variations in the input parameters.  
Hence, we allow for a slightly modified input set but in the same URL of POST /payouts

### 4.4.4. Designing a URL for a related and dependent entity

Another important entity in the payments ecosystem is **refund**. A refund is always linked to a payment. In fact a refund cannot exist without a payment.  
When designing URIs for refund, due to dependency on payment, ways to define it are as follows.

| /refunds | For *all* refunds |
| :---- | :---- |
| /payments/{payment\_id}/refunds | For refunds associated with a certain payment |
| /refunds?payment\_id={payment\_id} | For refunds associated with a certain payment |

The last two clearly provide refunds only in the context of that payment id.  
Whether you decide to also provide refunds independently as a resource is usually a product call. We of course expose refunds via /refunds URI too.  
In fact you can have both /refunds and /payments/{payment\_id}/refunds as both perform different functionality and it's perfectly legal. Another way to represent the latter is /refunds?payment\_id={payment\_id}. All these are essentially the same things expressed in different ways. For reasons we will document later, usually it's better to go with the last option compared to the second one when the first option is also live. But when the first option is not live, then the second option is the default option present.  
In the second option, refund becomes a sub-resource or sub-entity under payments. This is further covered in section [4.8. Sub-Entity](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#h.e1f947uyi9b3).

## **4.5. Dynamic sub-arrays**

In many cases, some attributes or fields can change depending on another field/value.  
A good example here is network based identifiers for different kinds of payments.  
For card payments, we get auth\_code and arn, whereas for netbanking payments, we need to expose bank\_payment\_id. For UPI, it's something else again.  
In case of payments, these values are primarily defined by external networks so we don't have much say in their creation beyond the decision whether to expose it or not via our APIs. In most cases here, that decision is usually made by our clients for us. So the only thing we really control in this specific example is how to expose them properly.  
There are two effective ways of exposing dynamic attributes in an entity. But the first rule is to always define dynamic attributes in a sub-array and not keep it in the root entity. The reason is that it helps you build the expectation in the documentation that the specific sub-array is meant to change dynamically based on certain conditions. The *conditions* aspect is really important here. And the conditions definition will mostly be in the root entity/array and not the sub-array.  
A good example of the above is the specific dynamic array acquirer\_data in the Payment entity. For every payment that happens, our upstream banks generate certain metadata like rrn or bank\_transaction\_id etc. But the data generated is specific to the payment method. Which means that data for upi is different from the data for cards. So, we have the dynamic array acquirer\_data which holds the method specific payment identifiers.  
So it looks as below:

| acquirer\_data: { "arn": "string", "rrn": "string", "utr": "string"} |
| :---- |

At a time, for cards, only arn is present, for UPI, rrn/arn can be present depending on the gateway. For E-mandate and instant refunds, UTR is present.  
We have very few dynamic arrays in API present in the company today. It will increase over time but should do at a slow pace. We definitely do not have a dynamic array present where it's not required at the moment.

### 4.5.1 Dynamic Keys and Values

We have seen a few examples where we would be required to expose multi level details to customers. The additional complexity is when the fields are high in number and parameters are expected to further increase frequently, making the keys and values more dynamic in nature.  
In such cases, we really need to be mindful of how to expose them effectively to our customers.  
The simple rule is to always define attributes in a way it is easy to search and build context.  
Below is an example of the way you can choose to expose certain fields,

|  "variables": \[   {     "type": "transaction",     "sub\_type": "payment",     "name": "debit" ,     "value": 50    } \] |
| :---- |

This has some benefits and advantages. Notably, it gives immense flexibility to the designer and the product teams because it's easy to stuff new values to the the type, sub\_type and name fields in future. And in complex scenarios, this keeps the schema future proof.  
However, this is not a good practice and should be very clearly avoided. Few reasons

* Navigability: In the above case, to find the actual value 50 here, the customer will have to process variables.type, variables.sub\_type, variables.name and then finally variables.value.  
* Readability: This json is more difficult to read than the above examples we have seen which are usually more straight-forward.  
* Scope misuse: It's easier to misuse this schema. Anyone can add any type, sub\_type to this in the future even if it's not directly related to the functionality provided by the api.

Notably, this structure usually comes through when the api designer is not fairly clear on the scope and is confused. Or is lazy to think through the current and future customer needs and design a schema which is more readable and future proof.  
The same schema can be better represented as below

|  "transaction": \[  {     "entity": "payment",     "debit": 50,  } \] |
| :---- |

Let’s expand on the above use case by taking a more realistic and a more complex example where merchants have multiple transactions across types such as payments, refunds, transfers, etc and each of these entities could have multiple transactions.  
If we follow the previous example, here’s how it would look like \-

|  "variables": \[   {     "type": "transaction",     "sub\_type": "payment",     "name": "credit",     "value": 50,   },     {     "type": "transaction",     "sub\_type": "payment",     "name": "credit",     "value": 30,   },     {     "type": "transaction",     "sub\_type": "refund",     "name": "debit",     "value": 30,   },     {     "type": "transaction",     "sub\_type": "transfer",     "name": "debit",     "value": 70,   },     {     "type": "transaction",     "sub\_type": "transfer",     "name": "debit",     "value": 70,       "id": "trf\_J4Zr75qK2sGfQq",   } \] |
| :---- |

Notice how the readability is getting impacted as more and more entities are being added to the API structure.    
Such a structure adds even more problems when we want to introduce more fields such as entity\_id, fee, tax, notes, etc. This structure doesn’t allow any easy mechanism for us to do so effectively. All values in the original example with type and sub\_type have to be a string by default due to the restriction of variability rather than being string or numeric or boolean or other object. Such schema doesn’t allow a richer expression of key types and value types that are permitted in JSON.  
Here is a better way to represent all this information \-

|   "transactions": \[   {     "entity\_id": "pay\_DEXrnipqTmWVGE",     "entity": "payment",     "debit": 0,     "credit": 97100,     "fee": 2900,     "tax": 0,     "notes": "Beam me up Scotty.",   },     {     "entity\_id": "pay\_BAXsqi1qTm1VfZ",     "entity": "payment",     "debit": 0,     "credit": 27310,     "fee": 1200,     "tax": 0,     "notes": "Beam me up Scotty 2.0",   },   {     "entity\_id": "rfnd\_DGRcGzZSLyEdg1",     "entity": "refund",     "debit": 242500,     "credit": 0,     "fee": 0,     "tax": 0,     "notes": "Beam me up Scotty 3.0",   },   {     "entity\_id": "trf\_DEUoCEtdsJgvl7",     "entity": "transfer",     "debit": 100296,     "credit": 0,     "fee": 296,     "tax": 46,     "description": null,   }   \]} |
| :---- |

Consider both the examples \- first one is forcing a hierarchy by default even when it’s not required, while we didn’t have to introduce unnecessary hierarchy in the second example, keeping the API design simple.  
Additionally, there is another way to club the different entities together to further enhance the searchability and readability of the API \-

| "transactions": {    "payments": \[     {    "id": "pay\_DEXrnipqTmWVGE",  "debit": 0,  "credit": 97100,  "fee": 2900,  "tax": 0,  "notes": "Beam me up Scotty." },     {         "id": "pay\_BAXsqi1qTm1VfZ",       "debit": 0,       "credit": 27310,       "fee": 1200,       "tax": 0,       "notes": "Beam me up Scotty 2.0",   }  \],"refunds": \[ {           "id": "rfnd\_DGRcGzZSLyEdg1",         "debit": 242500,         "credit": 0,         "fee": 0,         "tax": 0,         "notes": "Beam me up Scotty.", }     \],   "transfers": \[       {           "id": "trf\_DEUoCEtdsJgvl7",         "debit": 100296,         "credit": 0,         "fee": 296,         "tax": 46,         "description": null,       }    \]} |
| :---- |

In general, it’s a good idea to combine similar items together which are expected to be consumed together, such as in the above example.

## 4.6. Multiple Statuses (WIP)

Often you will come across situations where an entity has multiple status states which are not mutually exclusive. In payments for eg, a payment can be authorized and refunded or captured and partially refunded or partially captured and fully refunded. [\[l\]](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#cmnt12)[\[m\]](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#cmnt13)[\[n\]](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#cmnt14)[\[o\]](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#cmnt15)[\[p\]](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#cmnt16)  
Another eg, that we saw recently in terminals[\[q\]](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#cmnt17)[\[r\]](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#cmnt18) api

## 4.7. SHK's Axioms for defining a new API Entity

At various points, you will encounter a phase where you have to decide whether a sub-array should be better separated out into a separate entity/object. A lot of product managers believe this is a job best left to engineers. However, this needs to be completely owned by product managers even if engineers write/finalise this because it's the interface of the product. And it defines the workflow of the product.  
Few simple rules to follow when deciding a new entity creation are as follows \-

* Is there a real world corollary to it  
* Is there a separate lifecycle managing the states of the new entity which are independent from the main entity however influences it in one way or the other  
* Is the functionality in the new entity big enough that it's simpler to understand if we just carve it out separately. Big enough is of course subjective and cannot be defined exactly as of today.

If the answer to two of those three is yes, then it's probably better to design it as a separate entity linked to the main entity.  
An extremely good example of this is the subscription.registration entity. For cards, subscription registration happens instantly and with very simple binary outcomes without any intermediate steps. However, for eNACH, the registration step has its own full lifecycle with multiple operations happening in between. We ended up separating it as an entity from the main subscription and payment entities so that we do not need to keep piling unnecessary info about the registration event in payment entity. To be truthful it should have been a separate entity from the start itself but the card flow didn't necessarily translate well to the eNACH world. And we didn't possess the foresight to see it in the beginning.

## **4.8. Sub-Entity[\[s\]](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#cmnt19)**

Most of the companies providing APIs, only need to define approximately 5 or fewer number of entities. However, in a few cases, like Razorpay or Amazon, the product surface area is much larger. The number of APIs/Entities expands into more than a couple dozen over time. In this kind of scenario, namespacing becomes an important aspect. Specifically, the product should start being the root namespace, with the entities coming after it.  
In Razorpay context, today, payments/refunds/disputes etc. are independent entities. However, since payments acts as the main product, it may have made sense to name the entities as payment.refund, payment.dispute, etc. Another option would have been payment\_refund, the difference being that the separator is an underscore instead of a dot. However, we will also have entity names consisting of two words instead of one. For example, payment\_pages is a product with two words. In these cases, the words are joined by an underscore.  
In subscription, we have designed a registration entity and named it subscription.registration[\[t\]](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#cmnt20). Going forward, we should name the child entities of a product under the main namespace. The uri in that case will be /\<product\>/\<entity\>[\[u\]](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#cmnt21).

## 4.9. Querying and filters

For every entity, you will need to design the URL for fetching a collection of entities. This is pretty straightforward. If you have understood the doc this far, then you can guess the general pattern for this URL will be GET /entities.  
But this is not always sufficient. Let's take the use case of fetching payments. As we have described before, the URL for fetching payments in bulk will be GET /payments. This will give you a bunch of payments. But what if payments has a status field with certain values (let's say success/fail), and you only want to fetch payments with status success? For this, we will need to design query parameters… @todo finish this.  
Query parameters are a special functionality in URLs. To have a basic understanding of it, you can read the following Wikipedia entry \- [Query string](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Query_string)  
If you understand how query strings work then designing a query parameter for only fetching successful payments is very easy. You can simply do GET /payments?status=success. The key new element is the query string status=success at the end after the question-mark. This will tell the system to only return payments with status as success. You can also add other query parameters which will be helpful for users to return payments of specific variety. But don't add too many filters or any filter which can be confusing. General rule of thumb is to add a filter which is genuinely helpful and not just for the sake of completeness. More filters may increase flexibility but can also cause confusion.  
A special case of query parameters happen when you want to say fetch refunds of a specific payment. This scenario occurs when one entity has a one to many or many to one relationship with another entity. In case of payment and refund, one payment can have multiple refunds and each refund has a specific payment. Now, if you want to fetch refunds of a particular payment, there are usually two choices that are present. One is straightforward which you could have guessed by now \- GET /refunds?payment\_id=pay\_abcdef. The other option is GET /payments/pay\_abcdef/refunds. For a lot of people designing APIs, often the second option appears more appealing because it's a new URL and gives some definition to fetching refunds instead of it just being a query parameter. If you are designing the refund entity, then having more weight to an action via a separate URL may appear more appealing. However, there is a flipside to it. If there are multiple relationships of the refund entity with other entities, then you would need a separate URL for each of those and combining them will prove to be difficult. Here, we prefer to go with the first approach and keep all the filtering and querying limited to the single fetch URL. Note that this is a case of choice and we clearly align with one approach while other orgs may prefer another.  
Get refunds of a payment

| HTTP GET /refunds?payment\_id={payment\_id} |
| :---- |

### ***Note: In our docs, the url is listed as [https://api.razorpay.com/v1/payments/:id/refunds](https://api.razorpay.com/v1/payments/:id/refunds)**. This is an older url designed at the start of Razorpay when our principles were not well formed. The correct url remains the one highlighted in the doc.*

### 4.9.1 Querying with Complex Filters

There is a special case of querying when filters are way more complex and you may need to provide 4-5 fields with multiple values.  
Get by default doesn't support a body. Although the RFC doesn't forbid the body in GET explicitly, it's been a convention practice in the REST world as well as in the broader HTTP world that bodies in GET shouldn't be used for any functional purposes.  
Usually, there are good ways to do a query with just query params.  
But, exceptionally, in rare scenarios, you may be expected to pass more data and putting them in query param isn't a good option.  
In those cases, there is no standard convention worldwide which is elegant.  
At Razorpay, we are using the following pattern in those exceptional scenarios.

| HTTP POST /bankaccounts/retrieve {   "banks" : \[       {           "iin" : "606051"       },       {           "iin" : "607052"       }   \],  "device": {      "imei": "911489204188596",       "geocode": "72.9918372,19.1737834",      "os": "Android",          "os\_version": "5.1",        "ip": "10.193.72.15",      "app": "com.upi.axispay"  }} |
| :---- |

In the above example, you can see that we are passing the device data in the query itself as that's one of the requirements of the API. It's not very well designed, however we are limited in this instance by the design of the underlying gateway system which expects device data to be passed.  
Hence, to not pass all the semi-sensitive data in query params, we have deviated from the general GET query pattern for fetching data.  
Here, instead, we are defaulting to POST with a /entity/retrieve URL as a default. And all the parameters are then passed in the POST body as JSON. Note that the POST body isn't logged in the intermediary systems which handle the http request for processing and hence it's considered a safe option.

# **5\. Conventions**

If you are designing an API from scratch, then you need to be very careful about establishing conventions for use. Basic things like authentication, status codes, versioning etc. can be done in multiple ways but for one company's APIs, these things will be done in a singular manner. In the case of Razorpay, a lot of these conventions are already properly established and have been in use for 5+ years now. Changing those while not impossible, needs to be extremely thought through as it will affect a large number of integrations. What is important is that you understand the conventions extremely well along with the philosophy behind them. Most of the conventions can be understood by reading our api reference extensively \- [https://razorpay.com/docs/api](https://razorpay.com/docs/api).  
Beyond the http specific stuff and things documented above, few basic conventions present in Razorpay API by design are as follows \-

## 5.1. Common Attributes

Few common attributes that you will find in most Razorpay APIs are as below \-

### 5.1.1. Entity

All Razorpay objects are called entities. But that's not it, all the API responses carry an attribute entity with the value being the name of the API resource. It basically tells exactly what **entity** this object is.

### **5.1.2. Created At**

All Razorpay objects by default carry the created\_at attribute. It stores the timestamp at which the object was created.

### 5.1.3. Notes

Most Razorpay objects carry the sub-object notes which is meant for our customers to store metadata related to the object for their purposes. We shouldn't be using data stored in notes for any of our functionality and should always ensure it's only used for the purposes of our customers storing metadata. You can read more about notes here [Notes](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#h.uj1tha4qyt5t).  
Many of Razorpay's entities allow additional information to be stored in the notes object, in order to persist data that is relevant for the customer. It is not used by Razorpay for any operational purposes.  
The notes field is a key-value store and can have a maximum of 15 key-value pairs, each of 256 characters (maximum).  
For example, when initiating Payments, a business can store any reference id generated in notes. Additionally, notes is searchable on the dashboard by default giving the business additional flexibility to store any data it wants to search on. This has been a really helpful feature for a lot of our customers.  
*Note: we never attempted a search api for notes and the feature is only live on the dashboard. There are various challenges around designing a search api which merits serious debate and peer review whenever such an attempt is made and is not being detailed here at this point.*

### 5.1.4. Identifier

Most Razorpay objects are uniquely identified by their identifier which is mostly present in the attributed id. The identifier we generate is a 14 character alphanumeric case sensitive in nature. The id is generated as a combination of the timestamp of creation and a randomised value.  
All Razorpay entity identifiers are globally unique and collision free. This means that a payment identifier and a payout identifier and a customer identifier are all guaranteed to always be unique without any worry for collision. This has the added benefit that you can search an identifier in the logs and you will always get the correct entity back.  
Another important convention that we follow is that all Razorpay generated identifier values have a prefix attached which indicates the entity it belongs to. For example, for payment entity, we use the prefix `pay_` before the actual value. For customer identifiers, we use the prefix `cust_`. So, a payment identifier value can be `pay_BAXsqi1qTm1VfZ` and a customer identifier value can be `cust_BAXsqi1qDKFJDK.`

## 5.2. Attribute String Values

Just like attribute names are important, so are their values. We already talked about different kinds of data types in [3.6.3. Data Types](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#h.ovxvivknm0b2). Here, we will talk about conventions to keep in mind when designing these values of string type in a RESTFul API (especially from Razorpay perspective).  
All string values must be unequivocally and completely lowercase except in a few rare scenarios. If any value is not lowercase, then there has to be a good justification for it.  
For example, all our error reasons are completely specified in lower case. And if there are two words then they should be separated by an underscore \`\_\` as we called out in the attribute names section too.  
There are very few exceptions to this, like bank ifsc code. But that's an exception because ifsc codes are always written in uppercase.  
In other scenarios, if you see upper case values or capitalised values, then most likely they are mistakes of legacy designs.  
We could have gone with a different convention here, but what is most important here is to choose a convention that works (and is working well) and continue to stick to it.  
Note that this rule doesn't apply for free flowing text like description or similar such attributes where you are taking input from an end user in a long form text format.

## 5.3. Collection

A list of objects/entities is called collection. Usually, when doing a get on an API resource, when a multiple of entities is expected in results, then it's returned in a collection form.  
Check out our public docs as well \- [link](https://razorpay.com/docs/api/#collection-entity)  
The typical structure of a collection response is as follows \-

| { "count": 2, "entity": "collection", "items": \[   ...,   ... \]} |
| :---- |

 

## 5.4. Pagination

[https://razorpay.com/docs/api/\#pagination](https://razorpay.com/docs/api/#pagination)

## 5.5. Timestamp

For all Razorpay APIs, date and time are always depicted in [UNIX Epoch Timestamp](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Timestamp) format.  
The reason being that it's a universally accepted and easily interpreted format. There is a downside: it's not a good one to be human readable. But it's difficult to find a format which is both human and machine readable. And we went with one which is extremely easily machine readable and avoids most of the pitfalls that happen in date-time conversions.

## 5.6. Booleans

In a lot of cases, we will be defining boolean attributes where there are only two values possible \- true or false. An old but useful example of this is the captured attribute in Payments entity. It's value can only be true or false. And the input to it is provided in order entity by the payment\_capture field. The input values can be 0 or 1 as well as true or false. The input values are allowed to be of two different types (int and boolean) because many languages do not do a good job of type conversion when transforming to json especially when developer intent is not clear to ensure bool type transformation before json conversion. However, from a purist and correctness point of view, only boolean values are the right ones.  
Note that the provided output values should never be 0 or 1 because we already have defined boolean values of true and false. While 0 or 1 also have similar meaning they are not the best output values to provide in a boolean attribute case. And some languages can also interpret it as integer

## 5.7. Amount

The amount in all the APIs is always depicted in the lowest denomination format without any decimals. What this means is that instead of sending back 2.34 rupees in API response, we send back 234 (in paise). This takes care of all the division issues that can potentially occur and help us avoid floating point manipulation that can cause confusion for the client.  
Additionally, in certain cases, amount=0 is a valid input. For eg, when doing card authentication, we can do a 0 Rs authentication as well.  
In other cases, when amount is not a relevant field for a particular operation, then it's value should be null. For example, in the case of account validation, we do a Rs 1 bank account transfer. But in certain scenarios (like vpa validation), no amount is transferred. In this case, the amount value is null in the API response.  
In most of our APIs, null typically means not applicable in the current context.

## 5.9. Errors (WIP)

All successful API responses are returned with HTTP Status code 2xx. In case of failure, Razorpay API returns a JSON error response with the parameters that detail the reason for the failure.  
An error response in Razorpay world consists of code, description, field, source, step, reason and metadata parameters that help the customer diagnose and solve the error.  
You can read about errors in more detail here \- [https://razorpay.com/docs/errors/](https://razorpay.com/docs/errors/).  
An important attribute of error data is the error\_reason attribute. It contains the string error reason code that helps uniquely identify as to why the error has occurred.  
One thing to keep in mind when designing a new error reason is that for all internal errors specific to Razorpay, the error reason should always be "server\_error" only and nothing else. There is no benefit of giving visibility to a customer as to why there is an error in our system. We should try and reduce the errors to zero but if it doesn't happen, we should just throw server error. Some additional info can be helpful like when the system is down, but that gets taken care of by the relevant http status code and corresponding http response.

## 5.10. Expire By And Expire At (WIP)

This section is not refined, but just documenting discussion around having two timestamp attributes pertaining to user input vs the real time of action.  
...  
VAs need to have new attributes to allow the merchant to see how and when a VA is closed. There are quite a lot of options for naming these attributes, some of which have been used before for other products (payment pages and subscriptions have expired functionality). This section aims to summarize all discussions around the options.  
Need for two distinct attributes  
Initial discussions attempted to achieve multiple targets with one attribute, which proved difficult. In reality, there are two separate values to be tracked:  
The merchant-chosen configuration by which he wants the VA to close.  
The Razorpay-entered timestamp at which the VA actually closed.  
The two values would be different in many cases, eg. if a merchant creates a VA for a customer with the express purpose of collecting a single payment by the end of the month. In this case he’d set the value A to the end of the month, say 31st May. The customer makes the payment by 15th May however, and the merchant then closes the VA from the dashboard on 16th May. In this case value B gets set to 16th May. Both values are thus useful for the merchant, even after the closure.  
Naming of the two attributes  
The Razorpay-entered timestamp is easy to name. Since it tracks the time of occurrence of an event that has happened, there are a lot of corollaries in other entities, eg. settled\_at in transactions, created\_at in all entities, etc. Continuing this logic, we’re picking closed\_at for this.  
The merchant-entered key is slightly harder to name. Some of the top candidates are listed below:  
\- close\_at: There is nothing objectively wrong with this name, except that it is easy to confuse with closed\_at. The \_at suffix also gives the impression that this is the timestamp of an event that actually occurred, rather than being just a configured preference.  
\- closes\_at: The above problems exist here as well, i.e. easy to confuse (especially after the closure) and implies an event.  
\- close\_by: Initial objection to this name was that it implied a less-than-or-equal-to meaning, which didn’t work for actual closure events. However, after the separation into two separate attributes, this same meaning is beneficial, since the meaning we are going for is exactly that, i.e. if the merchant doesn’t close the VA himself, then the VA will auto-close by this time. Thus actual closing time is always less than or equal to the merchant-chosen close\_by time.  
With this reasoning, our final choices for the attributes are close\_by and closed\_at.[\[v\]](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#cmnt22)[\[w\]](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#cmnt23) Note that since this is coincidentally what was chosen in case of other entities as well (albeit without this level of discussion), we now have a chance to make all future entities consistent with regards to expire/close naming conventions. (edited)

## 5.11. Composite or Dependent Entity Creation

Usually, a RESTFul create operation should lead to one entity creation on Razorpay. For eg, creating a refund actually only creates a refund. However, there are situations where we need to create more than one entity via the same action and same or slightly different input. A classic example of this is payment via card. Another one will be a payment link which has a dependent entity creation for the entity customer. Let's look at a payment creation sample without card (say for wallet) \-

| {   "amount": 100,   "method": "wallet",   "wallet": "paytm",   "email": "shk@razorpay.com",   "contact": "+91-9384785373"} |
| :---- |

This will lead to one payment entity creation that is exposed to the customer.  
However, when creating a payment via card, we not only create the payment entity, but we also create the associated card entity. Normally this is done because the card itself has a lot of attributes which are independent from payment. Ideally, the workflow for this from the merchant side should have been two different operations, namely \-

* Create a card and get the card\_id  
* Create a payment using the previous card\_id

However, in reality, customers only want to fire one api call for a single operation (in this case payment). In the past, we have tried convincing clients to use multiple api calls in this situation but this rarely works. And we must accommodate the one api call request for these cases as is the demand from them.  
There are a couple of ways of solving this request with their own trade-offs and at the end I will highlight the Razorpay recommended way.

* **Composite API**[\[x\]](https://docs.google.com/document/d/1vufulBF4-LYNJCixZTKtiwy4UZcqO3zcE9-hNl0iwY4/mobilebasic#cmnt24)  
  In this case, we will take the card and

\<\<WIP\>\>

# 7\. Considerations when Designing a New API (WIP)

* Competition research  \- How have other competitors tackled this issue?  
* Multiple approaches \- Have you thought through at least 3 approaches to expose this offering via the API? Can you talk about the same?  
* Is this a brand new API v/s change to an existing API?  
* Can this be adopted without any or minimal change on the merchant side?  
* Is this thought through from a cross-method perspective?  
* Is this applicable across products?  
* Merchant facing doc

# 1\. Introduction

This document can be used as a common detailed source for the complete possible payload of the Orders and Payments API suite. While not all objects will be used by a single merchant in one use case, documenting them together here serves the following purposes:

* **For Product Managers/Engineering Managers:** This document is a starting point and source of truth for all existing fields, helping PMs/EMs plan and build their use cases. It ensures all teams can access the necessary information without referring to multiple public documentation pages.  
* **Promote Reuse of Artifacts:** By consolidating information, we encourage the reuse of artifacts across different use cases, improving efficiency and consistency.  
* **High-Level Overview:** It provides a clear guideline on what every existing object is expected to serve, offering a high-level view of “what goes where” in the API payload.

This document aims to centralise information, promote reuse, and provide clear guidance for all teams working with the Orders and Payments API.  
**Note** \- This document is for internal use only.

## 1.1. Legend

Follow the colour codes below to understand the sample payload (an example with values is given in the Specific Object & Use Cases section ).  
Green: type  
Purple : required/optional  
Red: possible values  
yellow : comment/guidelines for usage  
Sky blue : validations

# 2\. Orders API Suite

The Order Create API is a server-to-server (S2S) tool merchants use to communicate detailed order information to Razorpay. This API ensures that all essential details about an order are provided before the payment process begins, allowing for a smooth and efficient transaction. It includes information such as the order amount, currency, customer details, and other relevant data necessary for processing the payment.  
The Order API supports various use cases, each with its own public documentation. The Order Create Request payload is a JSON object sent to Razorpay's Order Create API to create a new order. It includes essential details like the amount, currency, and specific order information needed for Razorpay to process and manage the order effectively.

## 2.1. Order Create Request

### 2.1.2 Specific Objects & Use Cases

Below are specific object code samples along with their respective use cases. This table includes the object structures, descriptions of their functionalities, and examples of how they are utilized in different scenarios. Each entry provides insight into the purpose of the object, helping developers and product managers understand how to implement them effectively.  
*Note: Example values provided in  Green color.*

Scroll sideways to view the full table

| Object | Structure | Use Case | Guidance on usage |
| ----- | ----- | ----- | ----- |
| bank\_account | "bank\_account": {   "name": "Gaurav Kumar",   "ifsc": "HDFC0001234",   "account\_number": "1234567890" } | **Bank Account Collection and Validation**: This object is used to collect and validate bank account details when payments need to be processed through a third-party or specific bank account. It ensures that: **Bank Information**: Details like the bank name and IFSC code are correct. **Account Details**: The account number is valid and matches the provided bank information. **Payment Processing**: The payment is directed to the correct bank account, often required for third-party validations or specific payment routing. | Public |
| payment | "payment": { "capture": "string",         "capture\_options": {   "automatic\_expiry\_period": "int",      "manual\_expiry\_period": "int",                          "refund\_speed": "string"  },  "method": "string",  "payment keys": "payment values.."}, | **Order Creation**: When creating an order, this object specifies how the payment should be captured automatically. It includes settings for: **Automatic Capture**: How and when the payment should be automatically captured after the order is created. **Capture Options**: Details like expiry periods and refund speed related to automatic and manual captures. **Consolidated API**: When using an API that handles both order creation and payment processing together, this object provides the payment details required to complete the transaction. It includes: **Payment Method**: Specifies the type of payment method used. **Payment Payload**: Includes all necessary payment-related information for processing. | Public |
| payment\_config | "payment\_config": { "capture": "manual", "capture\_options": {   "automatic\_expiry\_period": 3600,   "manual\_expiry\_period": 7200,   "refund\_speed": "standard" }} | This object is used to set up rules for processing payments associated with an order. It helps determine: **Capture Method**: Whether payments should be captured automatically or manually. **Capture Options**: Includes settings for how long payments should wait before being captured and the speed at which refunds should be processed. | Public |
